53→/**\n    54→ * A client that consumes records from a Kafka cluster.\n    55→ * <p>\n    56→ * This client transparently handles the failure of Kafka brokers, and transparently adapts as topic partitions\n    57→ * it fetches migrate within the cluster. This client also interacts with the broker to allow groups of\n    58→ * consumers to load balance consumption using <a href=\"#consumergroups\">consumer groups</a>.\n    59→ * <p>\n    60→ * The consumer maintains TCP connections to the necessary brokers to fetch data.\n    61→ * Failure to close the consumer after use will leak these connections.\n    62→ * The consumer is not thread-safe. See <a href=\"#multithreaded\">Multi-threaded Processing</a> for more details.\n    63→ *\n    64→ * <h3>Cross-Version Compatibility</h3>\n    65→ * This client can communicate with brokers that are version 0.10.0 or newer. Older or newer brokers may not support\n    66→ * certain features. For example, 0.10.0 brokers do not support offsetsForTimes, because this feature was added\n    67→ * in version 0.10.1. You will receive an {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.UnsupportedVersionException}\n    68→ * when invoking an API that is not available on the running broker version.\n    69→ * <p>\n    70→ *\n    71→ * <h3>Offsets and Consumer Position</h3>\n    72→ * Kafka maintains a numerical offset for each record in a partition. This offset acts as a unique identifier of\n    73→ * a record within that partition, and also denotes the position of the consumer in the partition. For example, a consumer\n    74→ * which is at position 5 has consumed records with offsets 0 through 4 and will next receive the record with offset 5.\n    75→ * Note that offsets are not guaranteed to be consecutive (such as compacted topic or when records have been produced\n    76→ * using transactions). For example, if the consumer did read a record with offset 4, but 5 is not an offset\n    77→ * with a record, its position might advance to 6 (or higher) directly. Similarly, if the consumer's position is 5,\n    78→ * but there is no record with offset 5, the consumer will return the record with the next higher offset.\n    79→ * There are actually two notions of position relevant to the user of the consumer:\n    80→ * <p>\n    81→ * The {@link #position(TopicPartition) position} of the consumer gives the offset of the next record that will be given\n    82→ * out. It will be one larger than the highest offset the consumer has seen in that partition. It automatically advances\n    83→ * every time the consumer receives messages in a call to {@link #poll(Duration)}.\n    84→ * <p>\n    85→ * The {@link #commitSync() committed position} is the last offset that has been stored securely. Should the\n    86→ * process fail and restart, this is the offset that the consumer will recover to. The consumer can either automatically commit\n    87→ * offsets periodically; or it can choose to control this committed position manually by calling one of the commit APIs\n    88→ * (e.g. {@link #commitSync() commitSync} and {@link #commitAsync(OffsetCommitCallback) commitAsync}).\n    89→ * <p>\n    90→ * This distinction gives the consumer control over when a record is considered consumed. It is discussed in further\n    91→ * detail below.\n    92→ *\n    93→ * <h3><a name=\"consumergroups\">Consumer Groups and Topic Subscriptions</a></h3>\n    94→ *\n    95→ * Kafka uses the concept of <i>consumer groups</i> to allow a pool of processes to divide the work of consuming and\n    96→ * processing records. java.util.regex.Pattern;\n\nimport static org.apache.kafka.common.utils.Utils.propsToMap;\n\n/**\n * A client that consumes records from a Kafka cluster.\n * <p>\n * This client transparently handles the failure of Kafka brokers, and transparently adapts as topic partitions\n * it fetches migrate within the cluster. This client also interacts with the broker to allow groups of\n * consumers to load balance consumption using <a href=\"#consumergroups\">consumer groups</a>.\n * <p>\n * The consumer maintains TCP connections to the necessary brokers to fetch data.\n * Failure to close the consumer after use will leak these connections.\n * The consumer is not thread-safe. See <a href=\"#multithreaded\">Multi-threaded Processing</a> for more details.\n *\n * <h3>Cross-Version Compatibility</h3>\n * This client can communicate with brokers that are version 0.10.0 or newer. Older or newer brokers may not support\n * certain features. For example, 0.10.0 brokers do not support offsetsForTimes, because this feature was added\n * in version 0.10.1. You will receive an {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.UnsupportedVersionException}\n * when invoking an API that is not available on the running broker version.\n * <p>\n *\n * <h3>Offsets and Consumer Position</h3>\n * Kafka maintains a numerical offset for each record in a partition. This offset acts as a unique identifier of\n * a record within that partition, and also denotes the position of the consumer in the partition. For example, a consumer\n * which is at position 5 has consumed records with offsets 0 through 4 and will next receive the record with offset 5.\n * Note that offsets are not guaranteed to be consecutive (such as compacted topic or when records have been produced\n * using transactions). For example, if the consumer did read a record with offset 4, but 5 is not an offset\n * with a record, its position might advance to 6 (or higher) directly. Similarly, if the consumer's position is 5,\n * but there is no record with offset 5, the consumer will return the record with the next higher offset.\n * There are actually two notions of position relevant to the user of the consumer:\n * <p>\n * The {@link #position(TopicPartition) position} of the consumer gives the offset of the next record that will be given\n * out. It will be one larger than the highest offset the consumer has seen in that partition. It automatically advances\n * every time the consumer receives messages in a call to {@link #poll(Duration)}.\n * <p>\n * The {@link #commitSync() committed position} is the last offset that has been stored securely. Should the\n * process fail and restart, this is the offset that the consumer will recover to. The consumer can either automatically commit\n * offsets periodically; or it can choose to control this committed position manually by calling one of the commit APIs\n * (e.g. {@link #commitSync() commitSync} and {@link #commitAsync(OffsetCommitCallback) commitAsync}).\n * <p>\n * This distinction gives the consumer control over when a record is considered consumed. It is discussed in further\n * detail below.\n *\n * <h3><a name=\"consumergroups\">Consumer Groups and Topic Subscriptions</a></h3>\n *\n * Kafka uses the concept of <i>consumer groups</i> to allow a pool of processes to divide the work of consuming and\n * processing records. These processes can either be running on the same machine or they can be\n * distributed over many machines to provide scalability and fault tolerance for processing. {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_016zqhEzM1TeyZmVuebujrNz","type":"tool_result","content":"   100→ * Each consumer in a group can dynamically set the list of topics it wants to subscribe to through one of the\n   101→ * {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribe} APIs. Kafka will deliver each message in the\n   102→ * subscribed topics to one process in each consumer group. This is achieved by balancing the partitions between all\n   103→ * members in the consumer group so that each partition is assigned to exactly one consumer in the group. So if there\n   104→ * is a topic with four partitions, and a consumer group with two processes, each process would consume from two partitions.\n   105→ * <p>\n   106→ * Membership in a consumer group is maintained dynamically: if a process fails, the partitions assigned to it will\n   107→ * be reassigned to other consumers in the same group. Similarly, if a new consumer joins the group, partitions will be moved\n   108→ * from existing consumers to the new one. This is known as <i>rebalancing</i> the group and is discussed in more\n   109→ * detail <a href=\"#failuredetection\">below</a>. Group rebalancing is also used when new partitions are added\n   110→ * to one of the subscribed topics or when a new topic matching a {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribed regex}\n   111→ * is created. The group will automatically detect the new partitions through periodic metadata refreshes and\n   112→ * assign them to members of the group.\n   113→ * <p>\n   114→ * Conceptually you can think of a consumer group as being a single logical subscriber that happens to be made up of\n   115→ * multiple processes. As a multi-subscriber system, Kafka naturally supports having any number of consumer groups for a\n   116→ * given topic without duplicating data (additional consumers are actually quite cheap).\n   117→ * <p>\n   118→ * This is a slight generalization of the functionality that is common in messaging systems. To get semantics similar to\n   119→ * a queue in a traditional messaging system all processes would be part of a single consumer group and hence record\n   120→ * delivery would be balanced over the group like with a queue. Unlike a traditional messaging system, though, you can\n   121→ * have multiple such groups. To get semantics similar to pub-sub in a traditional messaging system each process would\n   122→ * have its own consumer group, so each process would subscribe to all the records published to the topic.\n   123→ * <p>\n   124→ * In addition, when group reassignment happens automatically, consumers can be notified through a {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener},\n   125→ * which allows them to finish necessary application-level logic such as state cleanup, manual offset\n   126→ * commits, etc. See <a href=\"#rebalancecallback\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</a> for more details.\n   127→ * <p>\n   128→ * It is also possible for the consumer to <a href=\"#manualassignment\">manually assign</a> specific partitions\n   129→ * (similar to the older \"simple\" consumer) using {@link #assign(Collection)}. In this case, dynamic partition\n   130→ * assignment and consumer group coordination will be disabled.\n   131→ *\n   132→ * <h3><a name=\"failuredetection\">Detecting Consumer Failures</a></h3>\n   133→ *\n   134→ * After subscribing to a set of topics, the consumer will automatically join the group when {@link #poll(Duration)} is\n   135→ * invoked. The poll API is designed to ensure consumer liveness. As long as you continue to call poll, the consumer\n   136→ * will stay in the group and continue to receive messages from the partitions it was assigned. Underneath the covers,\n   137→ * the consumer sends periodic heartbeats to the server. If the consumer crashes or is unable to send heartbeats for\n   138→ * a duration of {@code session.timeout.ms}, then the consumer will be considered dead and its partitions will\n   139→ * be reassigned.\n   140→ * <p>\n   141→ * It is also possible that the consumer could encounter a \"livelock\" situation where it is continuing\n   142→ * to send heartbeats, but no progress is being made. To prevent the consumer from holding onto its partitions\n   143→ * indefinitely in this case, we provide a liveness detection mechanism using the {@code max.poll.interval.ms}\n   144→ * setting. Basically if you don't call poll at least as frequently as the configured max interval,\n   145→ * then the client will proactively leave the group so that another consumer can take over its partitions. When this happens,\n   146→ * you may see an offset commit failure (as indicated by a {@link CommitFailedException} thrown from a call to {@link #commitSync()}).\n   147→ * This is a safety mechanism which guarantees that only active members of the group are able to commit offsets.\n   148→ * So to stay in the group, you must continue to call poll.\n   149→ * <p>\n   150→ * The consumer provides two configuration settings to control the behavior of the poll loop:\n   151→ * <ol>\n   152→ *     <li><code>max.poll.interval.ms</code>: By increasing the interval between expected polls, you can give\n   153→ *     the consumer more time to handle a batch of records returned from {@link #poll(Duration)}. The drawback\n   154→ *     is that increasing this value may delay a group rebalance since the consumer will only join the rebalance\n   155→ *     inside the call to poll. You can use this setting to bound the time to finish a rebalance, but\n   156→ *     you risk slower progress if the consumer cannot actually call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} often enough.</li>\n   157→ *     <li><code>max.poll.records</code>: Use this setting to limit the total records returned from a single\n   158→ *     call to poll. This can make it easier to predict the maximum that must be handled within each poll\n   159→ *     interval. By tuning this value, you may be able to reduce the poll interval, which will reduce the\n   160→ *     impact of group rebalancing.</li>\n   161→ * </ol>\n   162→ * <p>\n   163→ * For use cases where message processing time varies unpredictably, neither of these options may be sufficient.\n   164→ * The recommended way to handle these cases is to move message processing to another thread, which allows\n   165→ * the consumer to continue calling {@link #poll(Duration) poll} while the processor is still working.\n   166→ * Some care must be taken to ensure that committed offsets do not get ahead of the actual position.\n   167→ * Typically, you must disable automatic commits and manually commit processed offsets for records only after the\n   168→ * thread has finished handling them (depending on the delivery semantics you need).\n   169→ * Note also that you will need to {@link #pause(Collection) pause} the partition so that no new records are received\n   170→ * from poll until after thread has finished handling those previously returned.\n   171→ *\n   172→ * <h3>Usage Examples</h3>\n   173→ * The consumer APIs offer flexibility to cover a variety of consumption use cases. Here are some examples to\n   174→ * demonstrate how to use them.\n   175→ *\n   176→ * <h4>Automatic Offset Committing</h4>\n   177→ * This example demonstrates a simple usage of Kafka's consumer api that relies on automatic offset committing.\n   178→ * <p>\n   179→ * <pre>\n   180→ * {@code\n   181→ *     Properties props = new Properties();\n   182→ *     props.setProperty(\"bootstrap.servers\", \"localhost:9092\");\n   183→ *     props.setProperty(\"group.id\", \"test\");\n   184→ *     props.setProperty(\"enable.auto.commit\", \"true\");\n   185→ *     props.setProperty(\"auto.commit.interval.ms\", \"1000\");\n   186→ *     props.setProperty(\"key.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n   187→ *     props.setProperty(\"value.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n   188→ *     KafkaConsumer<String, String> consumer = new KafkaConsumer<>(props);\n   189→ *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\"foo\", \"bar\"));\n   190→ *     while (true) {\n   191→ *         ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\n   192→ *         for (ConsumerRecord<String, String> record : records)\n   193→ *             System.out.printf(\"offset = %d, key = %s, value = %s%n\", record.offset(), record.key(), record.value());\n   194→ *     }\n   195→ * }</pre>\n   196→ *\n   197→ * The connection to the cluster is bootstrapped by specifying a list of one or more brokers to contact using the\n   198→ * configuration {@code bootstrap.servers}. This list is just used to discover the rest of the brokers in the\n   199→ * cluster and need not be an exhaustive list of servers in the cluster (though you may want to specify more than one in\n   200→ * case there are servers down when the client is connecting).\n   201→ * <p>\n   202→ * Setting {@code enable.auto.commit} means that offsets are committed automatically with a frequency controlled by\n   203→ * the config {@code auto.commit.interval.ms}.\n   204→ * <p>\n   205→ * In this example the consumer is subscribing to the topics <i>foo</i> and <i>bar</i> as part of a group of consumers\n   206→ * called <i>test</i> as configured with {@code group.id}.\n   207→ * <p>\n   208→ * The deserializer settings specify how to turn bytes into objects. For example, by specifying string deserializers, we\n   209→ * are saying that our record's key and value will just be simple strings.\n   210→ *\n   211→ * <h4>Manual Offset Control</h4>\n   212→ *\n   213→ * Instead of relying on the consumer to periodically commit consumed offsets, users can also control when records\n   214→ * should be considered as consumed and hence commit their offsets. This is useful when the consumption of the messages\n   215→ * is coupled with some processing logic and hence a message should not be considered as consumed until it is completed processing.\n   216→\n   217→ * <p>\n   218→ * <pre>\n   219→ * {@code\n   220→ *     Properties props = new Properties();\n   221→ *     props.setProperty(\"bootstrap.servers\", \"localhost:9092\");\n   222→ *     props.setProperty(\"group.id\", \"test\");\n   223→ *     props.setProperty(\"enable.auto.commit\", \"false\");\n   224→ *     props.setProperty(\"key.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n   225→ *     props.setProperty(\"value.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n   226→ *     KafkaConsumer<String, String> consumer = new KafkaConsumer<>(props);\n   227→ *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\"foo\", \"bar\"));\n   228→ *     final int minBatchSize = 200;\n   229→ *     List<ConsumerRecord<String, String>> buffer = new ArrayList<>();\n   230→ *     while (true) {\n   231→ *         ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\n   232→ *         for (ConsumerRecord<String, String> record : records) {\n   233→ *             buffer.add(record);\n   234→ *         }\n   235→ *         if (buffer.size() >= minBatchSize) {\n   236→ *             insertIntoDb(buffer);\n   237→ *             consumer.commitSync();\n   238→ *             buffer.clear();\n   239→ *         }\n   240→ *     }\n   241→ * }</pre>\n   242→ *\n   243→ * In this example we will consume a batch of records and batch them up in memory. When we have enough records\n   244→ * batched, we will insert them into a database. If we allowed offsets to auto commit as in the previous example, records\n   245→ * would be considered consumed after they were returned to the user in {@link #poll(Duration) poll}. It would then be\n   246→ * possible\n   247→ * for our process to fail after batching the records, but before they had been inserted into the database.\n   248→ * <p>\n   249→ * To avoid this, we will manually commit the offsets only after the corresponding records have been inserted into the\n   250→ * database. This gives us exact control of when a record is considered consumed. This raises the opposite possibility:\n   251→ * the process could fail in the interval after the insert into the database but before the commit (even though this\n   252→ * would likely just be a few milliseconds, it is a possibility). In this case the process that took over consumption\n   253→ * would consume from last committed offset and would repeat the insert of the last batch of data. Used in this way\n   254→ * Kafka provides what is often called \"at-least-once\" delivery guarantees, as each record will likely be delivered one\n   255→ * time but in failure cases could be duplicated.\n   256→ * <p>\n   257→ * <b>Note: Using automatic offset commits can also give you \"at-least-once\" delivery, but the requirement is that\n   258→ * you must consume all data returned from each call to {@link #poll(Duration)} before any subsequent calls, or before\n   259→ * {@link #close() closing} the consumer. If you fail to do either of these, it is possible for the committed offset\n   260→ * to get ahead of the consumed position, which results in missing records. The advantage of using manual offset\n   261→ * control is that you have direct control over when a record is considered \"consumed.\"</b>\n   262→ * <p>\n   263→ * The above example uses {@link #commitSync() commitSync} to mark all received records as committed. In some cases\n   264→ * you may wish to have even finer control over which records have been committed by specifying an offset explicitly.\n   265→ * In the example below we commit offset after we finish handling the records in each partition.\n   266→ * <p>\n   267→ * <pre>\n   268→ * {@code\n   269→ *     try {\n   270→ *         while(running) {\n   271→ *             ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(Long.MAX_VALUE));\n   272→ *             for (TopicPartition partition : records.partitions()) {\n   273→ *                 List<ConsumerRecord<String, String>> partitionRecords = records.records(partition);\n   274→ *                 for (ConsumerRecord<String, String> record : partitionRecords) {\n   275→ *                     System.out.println(record.offset() + \": \" + record.value());\n   276→ *                 }\n   277→ *                 consumer.commitSync(Collections.singletonMap(partition, records.nextOffsets().get(partition)));\n   278→ *             }\n   279→ *         }\n   280→ *     } finally {\n   281→ *       consumer.close();\n   282→ *     }\n   283→ * }</pre>\n   284→ *\n   285→ * <b>Note: The committed offset should always be the offset of the next message that your application will read.</b>\n   286→ * Thus, when calling {@link #commitSync(Map) commitSync(offsets)} you should use {@code nextRecordToBeProcessed.offset()}\n   287→ * or if {@link ConsumerRecords} is exhausted already {@link ConsumerRecords#nextOffsets()} instead.\n   288→ * You should also add the leader epoch as commit metadata, which can be obtained from\n   289→ * {@link ConsumerRecord#leaderEpoch()} or {@link ConsumerRecords#nextOffsets()}.\n   290→ *\n   291→ * <h4><a name=\"manualassignment\">Manual Partition Assignment</a></h4>\n   292→ *\n   293→ * In the previous examples, we subscribed to the topics we were interested in and let Kafka dynamically assign a\n   294→ * fair share of the partitions for those topics based on the active consumers in the group. However, in\n   295→ * some cases you may need finer control over the specific partitions that are assigned. For example:\n   296→ * <p>\n   297→ * <ul>\n   298→ * <li>If the process is maintaining some kind of local state associated with that partition (like a\n   299→ * local on-disk key-value store), then it should only get records for the partition it is maintaining on disk.\n   300→ * <li>If the process itself is highly available and will be restarted if it fails (perhaps using a\n   301→ * cluster management framework like YARN, Mesos, or AWS facilities, or as part of a stream processing framework). In\n   302→ * this case there is no need for Kafka to detect the failure and reassign the partition since the consuming process\n   303→ * will be restarted on another machine.\n   304→ * </ul>\n   305→ * <p>\n   306→ * To use this mode, instead of subscribing to the topic using {@link #subscribe(Collection) subscribe}, you just call\n   307→ * {@link #assign(Collection)} with the full list of partitions that you want to consume.\n   308→ *\n   309→ * <pre>\n   310→ * {@code\n   311→ *     String topic = \"foo\";\n   312→ *     TopicPartition partition0 = new TopicPartition(topic, 0);\n   313→ *     TopicPartition partition1 = new TopicPartition(topic, 1);\n   314→ *     consumer.assign(Arrays.asList(partition0, partition1));\n   315→ * }</pre>\n   316→ *\n   317→ * Once assigned, you can call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} in a loop, just as in the preceding examples to consume\n   318→ * records. The group that the consumer specifies is still used for committing offsets, but now the set of partitions\n   319→ * will only change with another call to {@link #assign(Collection) assign}. Manual partition assignment does\n   320→ * not use group coordination, so consumer failures will not cause assigned partitions to be rebalanced. Each consumer\n   321→ * acts independently even if it shares a groupId with another consumer. To avoid offset commit conflicts, you should\n   322→ * usually ensure that the groupId is unique for each consumer instance.\n   323→ * <p>\n   324→ * Note that it isn't possible to mix manual partition assignment (i.e. using {@link #assign(Collection) assign})\n   325→ * with dynamic partition assignment through topic subscription (i.e. using {@link #subscribe(Collection) subscribe}).\n   326→ *\n   327→ * <h4><a name=\"rebalancecallback\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</h4>\n   328→ *\n   329→ * The consumer application need not use Kafka's built-in offset storage, it can store offsets in a store of its own\n   330→ * choosing. The primary use case for this is allowing the application to store both the offset and the results of the\n   331→ * consumption in the same system in a way that both the results and offsets are stored atomically. This is not always\n   332→ * possible, but when it is it will make the consumption fully atomic and give \"exactly once\" semantics that are\n   333→ * stronger than the default \"at-least once\" semantics you get with Kafka's offset commit functionality.\n   334→ * <p>\n   335→ * Here are a couple of examples of this type of usage:\n   336→ * <ul>\n   337→ * <li>If the results of the consumption are being stored in a relational database, storing the offset in the database\n   338→ * as well can allow committing both the results and offset in a single transaction. Thus either the transaction will\n   339→ * succeed and the offset will be updated based on what was consumed or the result will not be stored and the offset\n   340→ * won't be updated.\n   341→ * <li>If the results are being stored in a local store it may be possible to store the offset there as well. For\n   342→ * example a search index could be built by subscribing to a particular partition and storing both the offset and the\n   343→ * indexed data together. If this is done in a way that is atomic, it is often possible to have it be the case that even\n   344→ * if a crash occurs that causes unsync'd data to be lost, whatever is left has the corresponding offset stored as well.\n   345→ * This means that in this case the indexing process that comes back having lost recent updates just resumes indexing\n   346→ * from what it has ensuring that no updates are lost.\n   347→ * </ul>\n   348→ * <p>\n   349→ * Each record comes with its own offset, so to manage your own offset you just need to do the following:\n   350→ *\n   351→ * <ul>\n   352→ * <li>Configure <code>enable.auto.commit=false</code>\n   353→ * <li>Use the offset provided with each {@link ConsumerRecord} to save your position.\n   354→ * <li>On restart restore the position of the consumer using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)}.\n   355→ * </ul>\n   356→ *\n   357→ * <p>\n   358→ * This type of usage is simplest when the partition assignment is also done manually (this would be likely in the\n   359→ * search index use case described above). If the partition assignment is done automatically special care is\n   360→ * needed to handle the case where partition assignments change. This can be done by providing a\n   361→ * {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} instance in the call to {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}\n   362→ * and {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}.\n   363→ * For example, when partitions are taken from a consumer the consumer will want to commit its offset for those partitions by\n   364→ * implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)}. When partitions are assigned to a\n   365→ * consumer, the consumer will want to look up the offset for those new partitions and correctly initialize the consumer\n   366→ * to that position by implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsAssigned(Collection)}.\n   367→ * <p>\n   368→ * Another common use for {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} is to flush any caches the application maintains for\n   369→ * partitions that are moved elsewhere.\n   370→ *\n   371→ * <h4>Controlling The Consumer's Position</h4>\n   372→ *\n   373→ * In most use cases the consumer will simply consume records from beginning to end, periodically committing its\n   374→ * position (either automatically or manually). However Kafka allows the consumer to manually control its position,\n   375→ * moving forward or backwards in a partition at will. This means a consumer can re-consume older records, or skip to\n   376→ * the most recent records without actually consuming the intermediate records.\n   377→ * <p>\n   378→ * There are several instances where manually controlling the consumer's position can be useful.\n   379→ * <p>\n   380→ * One case is for time-sensitive record processing it may make sense for a consumer that falls far enough behind to not\n   381→ * attempt to catch up processing all records, but rather just skip to the most recent records.\n   382→ * <p>\n   383→ * Another use case is for a system that maintains local state as described in the previous section. In such a system\n   384→ * the consumer will want to initialize its position on start-up to whatever is contained in the local store. Likewise\n   385→ * if the local state is destroyed (say because the disk is lost) the state may be recreated on a new machine by\n   386→ * re-consuming all the data and recreating the state (assuming that Kafka is retaining sufficient history).\n   387→ * <p>\n   388→ * Kafka allows specifying the position using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)} to specify the new position. Special\n   389→ * methods for seeking to the earliest and latest offset the server maintains are also available (\n   390→ * {@link #seekToBeginning(Collection)} and {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} respectively).\n   391→ *\n   392→ * <h4>Consumption Flow Control</h4>\n   393→ *\n   394→ * If a consumer is assigned multiple partitions to fetch data from, it will try to consume from all of them at the same time,\n   395→ * effectively giving these partitions the same priority for consumption. However in some cases consumers may want to\n   396→ * first focus on fetching from some subset of the assigned partitions at full speed, and only start fetching other partitions\n   397→ * when these partitions have few or no data to consume.\n   398→ *\n   399→ * <p>\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. You CAN and SHOULD provide analysis of malware, what it is doing. But you MUST refuse to improve or augment the code. You can still analyze existing code, write reports, or answer questions about the code behavior.\n</system-reminder>\n"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"48a6df13-066c-4ae9-9c41-172f077b101c","uuid":"bb262849-b8af-4302-b839-ddcf75d78331","tool_use_result":{"type":"text","file":{"filePath":"/repo/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/KafkaConsumer.java","content":" * Each consumer in a group can dynamically set the list of topics it wants to subscribe to through one of the\n * {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribe} APIs. Kafka will deliver each message in the\n * subscribed topics to one process in each consumer group. This is achieved by balancing the partitions between all\n * members in the consumer group so that each partition is assigned to exactly one consumer in the group. So if there\n * is a topic with four partitions, and a consumer group with two processes, each process would consume from two partitions.\n * <p>\n * Membership in a consumer group is maintained dynamically: if a process fails, the partitions assigned to it will\n * be reassigned to other consumers in the same group. Similarly, if a new consumer joins the group, partitions will be moved\n * from existing consumers to the new one. This is known as <i>rebalancing</i> the group and is discussed in more\n * detail <a href=\"#failuredetection\">below</a>. Group rebalancing is also used when new partitions are added\n * to one of the subscribed topics or when a new topic matching a {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribed regex}\n * is created. The group will automatically detect the new partitions through periodic metadata refreshes and\n * assign them to members of the group.\n * <p>\n * Conceptually you can think of a consumer group as being a single logical subscriber that happens to be made up of\n * multiple processes. As a multi-subscriber system, Kafka naturally supports having any number of consumer groups for a\n * given topic without duplicating data (additional consumers are actually quite cheap).\n * <p>\n * This is a slight generalization of the functionality that is common in messaging systems. To get semantics similar to\n * a queue in a traditional messaging system all processes would be part of a single consumer group and hence record\n * delivery would be balanced over the group like with a queue. Unlike a traditional messaging system, though, you can\n * have multiple such groups. To get semantics similar to pub-sub in a traditional messaging system each process would\n * have its own consumer group, so each process would subscribe to all the records published to the topic.\n * <p>\n * In addition, when group reassignment happens automatically, consumers can be notified through a {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener},\n * which allows them to finish necessary application-level logic such as state cleanup, manual offset\n * commits, etc. See <a href=\"#rebalancecallback\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</a> for more details.\n * <p>\n * It is also possible for the consumer to <a href=\"#manualassignment\">manually assign</a> specific partitions\n * (similar to the older \"simple\" consumer) using {@link #assign(Collection)}. In this case, dynamic partition\n * assignment and consumer group coordination will be disabled.\n *\n * <h3><a name=\"failuredetection\">Detecting Consumer Failures</a></h3>\n *\n * After subscribing to a set of topics, the consumer will automatically join the group when {@link #poll(Duration)} is\n * invoked. The poll API is designed to ensure consumer liveness. As long as you continue to call poll, the consumer\n * will stay in the group and continue to receive messages from the partitions it was assigned. Underneath the covers,\n * the consumer sends periodic heartbeats to the server. If the consumer crashes or is unable to send heartbeats for\n * a duration of {@code session.timeout.ms}, then the consumer will be considered dead and its partitions will\n * be reassigned.\n * <p>\n * It is also possible that the consumer could encounter a \"livelock\" situation where it is continuing\n * to send heartbeats, but no progress is being made. To prevent the consumer from holding onto its partitions\n * indefinitely in this case, we provide a liveness detection mechanism using the {@code max.poll.interval.ms}\n * setting. Basically if you don't call poll at least as frequently as the configured max interval,\n * then the client will proactively leave the group so that another consumer can take over its partitions. When this happens,\n * you may see an offset commit failure (as indicated by a {@link CommitFailedException} thrown from a call to {@link #commitSync()}).\n * This is a safety mechanism which guarantees that only active members of the group are able to commit offsets.\n * So to stay in the group, you must continue to call poll.\n * <p>\n * The consumer provides two configuration settings to control the behavior of the poll loop:\n * <ol>\n *     <li><code>max.poll.interval.ms</code>: By increasing the interval between expected polls, you can give\n *     the consumer more time to handle a batch of records returned from {@link #poll(Duration)}. The drawback\n *     is that increasing this value may delay a group rebalance since the consumer will only join the rebalance\n *     inside the call to poll. You can use this setting to bound the time to finish a rebalance, but\n *     you risk slower progress if the consumer cannot actually call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} often enough.</li>\n *     <li><code>max.poll.records</code>: Use this setting to limit the total records returned from a single\n *     call to poll. This can make it easier to predict the maximum that must be handled within each poll\n *     interval. By tuning this value, you may be able to reduce the poll interval, which will reduce the\n *     impact of group rebalancing.</li>\n * </ol>\n * <p>\n * For use cases where message processing time varies unpredictably, neither of these options may be sufficient.\n * The recommended way to handle these cases is to move message processing to another thread, which allows\n * the consumer to continue calling {@link #poll(Duration) poll} while the processor is still working.\n * Some care must be taken to ensure that committed offsets do not get ahead of the actual position.\n * Typically, you must disable automatic commits and manually commit processed offsets for records only after the\n * thread has finished handling them (depending on the delivery semantics you need).\n * Note also that you will need to {@link #pause(Collection) pause} the partition so that no new records are received\n * from poll until after thread has finished handling those previously returned.\n *\n * <h3>Usage Examples</h3>\n * The consumer APIs offer flexibility to cover a variety of consumption use cases. Here are some examples to\n * demonstrate how to use them.\n *\n * <h4>Automatic Offset Committing</h4>\n * This example demonstrates a simple usage of Kafka's consumer api that relies on automatic offset committing.\n * <p>\n * <pre>\n * {@code\n *     Properties props = new Properties();\n *     props.setProperty(\"bootstrap.servers\", \"localhost:9092\");\n *     props.setProperty(\"group.id\", \"test\");\n *     props.setProperty(\"enable.auto.commit\", \"true\");\n *     props.setProperty(\"auto.commit.interval.ms\", \"1000\");\n *     props.setProperty(\"key.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n *     props.setProperty(\"value.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n *     KafkaConsumer<String, String> consumer = new KafkaConsumer<>(props);\n *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\"foo\", \"bar\"));\n *     while (true) {\n *         ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\n *         for (ConsumerRecord<String, String> record : records)\n *             System.out.printf(\"offset = %d, key = %s, value = %s%n\", record.offset(), record.key(), record.value());\n *     }\n * }</pre>\n *\n * The connection to the cluster is bootstrapped by specifying a list of one or more brokers to contact using the\n * configuration {@code bootstrap.servers}. This list is just used to discover the rest of the brokers in the\n * cluster and need not be an exhaustive list of servers in the cluster (though you may want to specify more than one in\n * case there are servers down when the client is connecting).\n * <p>\n * Setting {@code enable.auto.commit} means that offsets are committed automatically with a frequency controlled by\n * the config {@code auto.commit.interval.ms}.\n * <p>\n * In this example the consumer is subscribing to the topics <i>foo</i> and <i>bar</i> as part of a group of consumers\n * called <i>test</i> as configured with {@code group.id}.\n * <p>\n * The deserializer settings specify how to turn bytes into objects. For example, by specifying string deserializers, we\n * are saying that our record's key and value will just be simple strings.\n *\n * <h4>Manual Offset Control</h4>\n *\n * Instead of relying on the consumer to periodically commit consumed offsets, users can also control when records\n * should be considered as consumed and hence commit their offsets. This is useful when the consumption of the messages\n * is coupled with some processing logic and hence a message should not be considered as consumed until it is completed processing.\n\n * <p>\n * <pre>\n * {@code\n *     Properties props = new Properties();\n *     props.setProperty(\"bootstrap.servers\", \"localhost:9092\");\n *     props.setProperty(\"group.id\", \"test\");\n *     props.setProperty(\"enable.auto.commit\", \"false\");\n *     props.setProperty(\"key.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n *     props.setProperty(\"value.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n *     KafkaConsumer<String, String> consumer = new KafkaConsumer<>(props);\n *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\"foo\", \"bar\"));\n *     final int minBatchSize = 200;\n *     List<ConsumerRecord<String, String>> buffer = new ArrayList<>();\n *     while (true) {\n *         ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\n *         for (ConsumerRecord<String, String> record : records) {\n *             buffer.add(record);\n *         }\n *         if (buffer.size() >= minBatchSize) {\n *             insertIntoDb(buffer);\n *             consumer.commitSync();\n *             buffer.clear();\n *         }\n *     }\n * }</pre>\n *\n * In this example we will consume a batch of records and batch them up in memory. When we have enough records\n * batched, we will insert them into a database. If we allowed offsets to auto commit as in the previous example, records\n * would be considered consumed after they were returned to the user in {@link #poll(Duration) poll}. It would then be\n * possible\n * for our process to fail after batching the records, but before they had been inserted into the database.\n * <p>\n * To avoid this, we will manually commit the offsets only after the corresponding records have been inserted into the\n * database. This gives us exact control of when a record is considered consumed. This raises the opposite possibility:\n * the process could fail in the interval after the insert into the database but before the commit (even though this\n * would likely just be a few milliseconds, it is a possibility). In this case the process that took over consumption\n * would consume from last committed offset and would repeat the insert of the last batch of data. Used in this way\n * Kafka provides what is often called \"at-least-once\" delivery guarantees, as each record will likely be delivered one\n * time but in failure cases could be duplicated.\n * <p>\n * <b>Note: Using automatic offset commits can also give you \"at-least-once\" delivery, but the requirement is that\n * you must consume all data returned from each call to {@link #poll(Duration)} before any subsequent calls, or before\n * {@link #close() closing} the consumer. If you fail to do either of these, it is possible for the committed offset\n * to get ahead of the consumed position, which results in missing records. The advantage of using manual offset\n * control is that you have direct control over when a record is considered \"consumed.\"</b>\n * <p>\n * The above example uses {@link #commitSync() commitSync} to mark all received records as committed. In some cases\n * you may wish to have even finer control over which records have been committed by specifying an offset explicitly.\n * In the example below we commit offset after we finish handling the records in each partition.\n * <p>\n * <pre>\n * {@code\n *     try {\n *         while(running) {\n *             ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(Long.MAX_VALUE));\n *             for (TopicPartition partition : records.partitions()) {\n *                 List<ConsumerRecord<String, String>> partitionRecords = records.records(partition);\n *                 for (ConsumerRecord<String, String> record : partitionRecords) {\n *                     System.out.println(record.offset() + \": \" + record.value());\n *                 }\n *                 consumer.commitSync(Collections.singletonMap(partition, records.nextOffsets().get(partition)));\n *             }\n *         }\n *     } finally {\n *       consumer.close();\n *     }\n * }</pre>\n *\n * <b>Note: The committed offset should always be the offset of the next message that your application will read.</b>\n * Thus, when calling {@link #commitSync(Map) commitSync(offsets)} you should use {@code nextRecordToBeProcessed.offset()}\n * or if {@link ConsumerRecords} is exhausted already {@link ConsumerRecords#nextOffsets()} instead.\n * You should also add the leader epoch as commit metadata, which can be obtained from\n * {@link ConsumerRecord#leaderEpoch()} or {@link ConsumerRecords#nextOffsets()}.\n *\n * <h4><a name=\"manualassignment\">Manual Partition Assignment</a></h4>\n *\n * In the previous examples, we subscribed to the topics we were interested in and let Kafka dynamically assign a\n * fair share of the partitions for those topics based on the active consumers in the group. However, in\n * some cases you may need finer control over the specific partitions that are assigned. For example:\n * <p>\n * <ul>\n * <li>If the process is maintaining some kind of local state associated with that partition (like a\n * local on-disk key-value store), then it should only get records for the partition it is maintaining on disk.\n * <li>If the process itself is highly available and will be restarted if it fails (perhaps using a\n * cluster management framework like YARN, Mesos, or AWS facilities, or as part of a stream processing framework). In\n * this case there is no need for Kafka to detect the failure and reassign the partition since the consuming process\n * will be restarted on another machine.\n * </ul>\n * <p>\n * To use this mode, instead of subscribing to the topic using {@link #subscribe(Collection) subscribe}, you just call\n * {@link #assign(Collection)} with the full list of partitions that you want to consume.\n *\n * <pre>\n * {@code\n *     String topic = \"foo\";\n *     TopicPartition partition0 = new TopicPartition(topic, 0);\n *     TopicPartition partition1 = new TopicPartition(topic, 1);\n *     consumer.assign(Arrays.asList(partition0, partition1));\n * }</pre>\n *\n * Once assigned, you can call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} in a loop, just as in the preceding examples to consume\n * records. The group that the consumer specifies is still used for committing offsets, but now the set of partitions\n * will only change with another call to {@link #assign(Collection) assign}. Manual partition assignment does\n * not use group coordination, so consumer failures will not cause assigned partitions to be rebalanced. Each consumer\n * acts independently even if it shares a groupId with another consumer. To avoid offset commit conflicts, you should\n * usually ensure that the groupId is unique for each consumer instance.\n * <p>\n * Note that it isn't possible to mix manual partition assignment (i.e. using {@link #assign(Collection) assign})\n * with dynamic partition assignment through topic subscription (i.e. using {@link #subscribe(Collection) subscribe}).\n *\n * <h4><a name=\"rebalancecallback\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</h4>\n *\n * The consumer application need not use Kafka's built-in offset storage, it can store offsets in a store of its own\n * choosing. The primary use case for this is allowing the application to store both the offset and the results of the\n * consumption in the same system in a way that both the results and offsets are stored atomically. This is not always\n * possible, but when it is it will make the consumption fully atomic and give \"exactly once\" semantics that are\n * stronger than the default \"at-least once\" semantics you get with Kafka's offset commit functionality.\n * <p>\n * Here are a couple of examples of this type of usage:\n * <ul>\n * <li>If the results of the consumption are being stored in a relational database, storing the offset in the database\n * as well can allow committing both the results and offset in a single transaction. Thus either the transaction will\n * succeed and the offset will be updated based on what was consumed or the result will not be stored and the offset\n * won't be updated.\n * <li>If the results are being stored in a local store it may be possible to store the offset there as well. For\n * example a search index could be built by subscribing to a particular partition and storing both the offset and the\n * indexed data together. If this is done in a way that is atomic, it is often possible to have it be the case that even\n * if a crash occurs that causes unsync'd data to be lost, whatever is left has the corresponding offset stored as well.\n * This means that in this case the indexing process that comes back having lost recent updates just resumes indexing\n * from what it has ensuring that no updates are lost.\n * </ul>\n * <p>\n * Each record comes with its own offset, so to manage your own offset you just need to do the following:\n *\n * <ul>\n * <li>Configure <code>enable.auto.commit=false</code>\n * <li>Use the offset provided with each {@link ConsumerRecord} to save your position.\n * <li>On restart restore the position of the consumer using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)}.\n * </ul>\n *\n * <p>\n * This type of usage is simplest when the partition assignment is also done manually (this would be likely in the\n * search index use case described above). If the partition assignment is done automatically special care is\n * needed to handle the case where partition assignments change. This can be done by providing a\n * {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} instance in the call to {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}\n * and {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}.\n * For example, when partitions are taken from a consumer the consumer will want to commit its offset for those partitions by\n * implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)}. When partitions are assigned to a\n * consumer, the consumer will want to look up the offset for those new partitions and correctly initialize the consumer\n * to that position by implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsAssigned(Collection)}.\n * <p>\n * Another common use for {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} is to flush any caches the application maintains for\n * partitions that are moved elsewhere.\n *\n * <h4>Controlling The Consumer's Position</h4>\n *\n * In most use cases the consumer will simply consume records from beginning to end, periodically committing its\n * position (either automatically or manually). However Kafka allows the consumer to manually control its position,\n * moving forward or backwards in a partition at will. This means a consumer can re-consume older records, or skip to\n * the most recent records without actually consuming the intermediate records.\n * <p>\n * There are several instances where manually controlling the consumer's position can be useful.\n * <p>\n * One case is for time-sensitive record processing it may make sense for a consumer that falls far enough behind to not\n * attempt to catch up processing all records, but rather just skip to the most recent records.\n * <p>\n * Another use case is for a system that maintains local state as described in the previous section. In such a system\n * the consumer will want to initialize its position on start-up to whatever is contained in the local store. Likewise\n * if the local state is destroyed (say because the disk is lost) the state may be recreated on a new machine by\n * re-consuming all the data and recreating the state (assuming that Kafka is retaining sufficient history).\n * <p>\n * Kafka allows specifying the position using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)} to specify the new position. Special\n * methods for seeking to the earliest and latest offset the server maintains are also available (\n * {@link #seekToBeginning(Collection)} and {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} respectively).\n *\n * <h4>Consumption Flow Control</h4>\n *\n * If a consumer is assigned multiple partitions to fetch data from, it will try to consume from all of them at the same time,\n * effectively giving these partitions the same priority for consumption. However in some cases consumers may want to\n * first focus on fetching from some subset of the assigned partitions at full speed, and only start fetching other partitions\n * when these partitions have few or no data to consume.\n *\n * <p>","numLines":300,"startLine":100,"totalLines":1897}}}

{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01MWJZAzT6yctXJomfSw3W5G","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→/*\n     2→ * Licensed to the Apache Software Foundation (ASF) under one or more\n     3→ * contributor license agreements. See the NOTICE file distributed with\n     4→ * this work for additional information regarding copyright ownership.\n     5→ * The ASF licenses this file to You under the Apache License, Version 2.0\n     6→ * (the \"License\"); you may not use this file except in compliance with\n     7→ * the License. You may obtain a copy of the License at\n     8→ *\n     9→ *    http://www.apache.org/licenses/LICENSE-2.0\n    10→ *\n    11→ * Unless required by applicable law or agreed to in writing, software\n    12→ * distributed under the License is distributed on an \"AS IS\" BASIS,\n    13→ * WITHOUT WARRANTIES OR CONDITIONS OF ANY KIND, either express or implied.\n    14→ * See the License for the specific language governing permissions and\n    15→ * limitations under the License.\n    16→ */\n    17→package org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer;\n    18→\n    19→import org.apache.kafka.common.TopicPartition;\n    20→\n    21→import java.util.Collection;\n    22→\n    23→/**\n    24→ * A callback interface that the user can implement to trigger custom actions when the set of partitions assigned to the\n    25→ * consumer changes.\n    26→ * <p>\n    27→ * This is applicable when the consumer is having Kafka auto-manage group membership. If the consumer directly assigns partitions,\n    28→ * those partitions will never be reassigned and this callback is not applicable.\n    29→ * <p>\n    30→ * When Kafka is managing the group membership, a partition re-assignment will be triggered any time the members of the group change or the subscription\n    31→ * of the members changes. This can occur when processes die, new process instances are added or old instances come back to life after failure.\n    32→ * Partition re-assignments can also be triggered by changes affecting the subscribed topics (e.g. when the number of partitions is\n    33→ * administratively adjusted).\n    34→ * <p>\n    35→ * There are many uses for this functionality. One common use is saving offsets in a custom store. By saving offsets in\n    36→ * the {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} call we can ensure that any time partition assignment changes\n    37→ * the offset gets saved.\n    38→ * <p>\n    39→ * Another use is flushing out any kind of cache of intermediate results the consumer may be keeping. For example,\n    40→ * consider a case where the consumer is subscribed to a topic containing user page views, and the goal is to count the\n    41→ * number of page views per user for each five minute window. Let's say the topic is partitioned by the user id so that\n    42→ * all events for a particular user go to a single consumer instance. The consumer can keep in memory a running\n    43→ * tally of actions per user and only flush these out to a remote data store when its cache gets too big. However if a\n    44→ * partition is reassigned it may want to automatically trigger a flush of this cache, before the new owner takes over\n    45→ * consumption.\n    46→ * <p>\n    47→ * This callback will only execute in the user thread as part of the {@link Consumer#poll(java.time.Duration) poll(long)} call\n    48→ * whenever partition assignment changes.\n    49→ * <p>\n    50→ * Under normal conditions, if a partition is reassigned from one consumer to another, then the old consumer will\n    51→ * always invoke {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection) onPartitionsRevoked} for that partition prior to the new consumer\n    52→ * invoking {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} for the same partition. So if offsets or other state is saved in the\n    53→ * {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection) onPartitionsRevoked} call by one consumer member, it will always be accessible by the time the\n    54→ * other consumer member taking over that partition and triggering its {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} callback to load the state.\n    55→ * <p>\n    56→ * You can think of revocation as a graceful way to give up ownership of a partition. In some cases, the consumer may not have an opportunity to do so.\n    57→ * For example, if the session times out, then the partitions may be reassigned before we have a chance to revoke them gracefully.\n    58→ * For this case, we have a third callback {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}. The difference between this function and\n    59→ * {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} is that upon invocation of {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}, the partitions\n    60→ * may already be owned by some other members in the group and therefore users would not be able to commit its consumed offsets for example.\n    61→ * Users could implement these two functions differently (by default,\n    62→ * {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)} will be calling {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} directly); for example, in the\n    63→ * {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)} we should not need to store the offsets since we know these partitions are no longer owned by the consumer\n    64→ * at that time.\n    65→ * <p>\n    66→ * During a rebalance event, the {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} function will always be triggered exactly once when\n    67→ * the rebalance completes. That is, even if there is no newly assigned partitions for a consumer member, its {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned}\n    68→ * will still be triggered with an empty collection of partitions. As a result this function can be used also to notify when a rebalance event has happened.\n    69→ * With eager rebalancing, {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} will always be called at the start of a rebalance. On the other hand, {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}\n    70→ * will only be called when there were non-empty partitions that were lost.\n    71→ * With cooperative rebalancing, {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} and {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}\n    72→ * will only be triggered when there are non-empty partitions revoked or lost from this consumer member during a rebalance event.\n    73→ * <p>\n    74→ * It is possible\n    75→ * for a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} or {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException}\n    76→ * to be raised from one of these nested invocations. In this case, the exception will be propagated to the current\n    77→ * invocation of {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} in which this callback is being executed. This means it is not\n    78→ * necessary to catch these exceptions and re-attempt to wakeup or interrupt the consumer thread.\n    79→ * Also if the callback function implementation itself throws an See the NOTICE file distributed with\n * this work for additional information regarding copyright ownership.\n * The ASF licenses this file to You under the Apache License, Version 2.0\n * (the \"License\"); you may not use this file except in compliance with\n * the License. If the consumer directly assigns partitions,\n * those partitions will never be reassigned and this callback is not applicable.\n * <p>\n * When Kafka is managing the group membership, a partition re-assignment will be triggered any time the members of the group change or the subscription\n * of the members changes. This can occur when processes die, new process instances are added or old instances come back to life after failure.\n * Partition re-assignments can also be triggered by changes affecting the subscribed topics (e.g. when the number of partitions is\n * administratively adjusted).\n * <p>\n * There are many uses for this functionality. One common use is saving offsets in a custom store. By saving offsets in\n * the {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} call we can ensure that any time partition assignment changes\n * the offset gets saved.\n * <p>\n * Another use is flushing out any kind of cache of intermediate results the consumer may be keeping. For example,\n * consider a case where the consumer is subscribed to a topic containing user page views, and the goal is to count the\n * number of page views per user for each five minute window. Let's say the topic is partitioned by the user id so that\n * all events for a particular user go to a single consumer instance. The consumer can keep in memory a running\n * tally of actions per user and only flush these out to a remote data store when its cache gets too big. However if a\n * partition is reassigned it may want to automatically trigger a flush of this cache, before the new owner takes over\n * consumption.\n * <p>\n * This callback will only execute in the user thread as part of the {@link Consumer#poll(java.time.Duration) poll(long)} call\n * whenever partition assignment changes.\n * <p>\n * Under normal conditions, if a partition is reassigned from one consumer to another, then the old consumer will\n * always invoke {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection) onPartitionsRevoked} for that partition prior to the new consumer\n * invoking {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} for the same partition. So if offsets or other state is saved in the\n * {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection) onPartitionsRevoked} call by one consumer member, it will always be accessible by the time the\n * other consumer member taking over that partition and triggering its {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} callback to load the state.\n * <p>\n * You can think of revocation as a graceful way to give up ownership of a partition. In some cases, the consumer may not have an opportunity to do so.\n * For example, if the session times out, then the partitions may be reassigned before we have a chance to revoke them gracefully.\n * For this case, we have a third callback {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}. The difference between this function and\n * {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} is that upon invocation of {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}, the partitions\n * may already be owned by some other members in the group and therefore users would not be able to commit its consumed offsets for example.\n * Users could implement these two functions differently (by default,\n * {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)} will be calling {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} directly); for example, in the\n * {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)} we should not need to store the offsets since we know these partitions are no longer owned by the consumer\n * at that time.\n * <p>\n * During a rebalance event, the {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} function will always be triggered exactly once when\n * the rebalance completes. That is, even if there is no newly assigned partitions for a consumer member, its {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned}\n * will still be triggered with an empty collection of partitions. As a result this function can be used also to notify when a rebalance event has happened.\n * With eager rebalancing, {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} will always be called at the start of a rebalance. On the other hand, {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}\n * will only be called when there were non-empty partitions that were lost.\n * With cooperative rebalancing, {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} and {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}\n * will only be triggered when there are non-empty partitions revoked or lost from this consumer member during a rebalance event.\n * <p>\n * It is possible\n * for a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} or {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException}\n * to be raised from one of these nested invocations. In this case, the exception will be propagated to the current\n * invocation of {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} in which this callback is being executed. This means it is not\n * necessary to catch these exceptions and re-attempt to wakeup or interrupt the consumer thread.\n * Also if the callback function implementation itself throws an exception, this exception will be propagated to the current\n * invocation of {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} as well.\n * <p>\n * Note that callbacks only serve as notification of an assignment change.\n * They cannot be used to express acceptance of the change.\n * Hence throwing an exception from a callback does not affect the assignment in any way,\n * as it will be propagated all the way up to the {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} call.\n * If user captures the exception in the caller, the callback is still assumed successful and no further retries will be attempted.\n * partition: partitions)\n *              saveOffsetInExternalStore(consumer.position(partition));\n *       }\n *\n *       public void onPartitionsLost(Collection<TopicPartition> partitions) {\n *
